Goodrow v. Lane Bryant, Inc.


SJC-08241, SJC-08189


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


432 Mass. 165; 732 N.E.2d 289; 2000 Mass. LEXIS 397


April 6, 2000, Argued  
July 31, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on July 7, 1995. The case was heard by Allan van Gestel, J., and a motion to file a late notice of cross-appeal was heard in the Appeals Court by Kenneth Laurence, J. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.



DISPOSITION: Judgment in favor of Goodrow reversed and judgment for Lane Bryant is to enter therein. The judgment denying punitive damages affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant store appealed a judgment from the Superior Court Department (Massachusetts), finding in favor of plaintiff employee in her claim that she was not exempt from overtime provisions under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 151, § 1A. Plaintiff cross appealed the denial of triple damages pursuant to Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 151, § 1B.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff commenced a class action against defendant store for failing to pay overtime compensation at the rate of 1 1/2 times her hourly rate for the hours she worked in excess of 40 in any work week, contrary to Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 151, § 1A. She also sought individually triple damages under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 151, § 1B. After a jury-waived trial, a judge found for plaintiff. Defendant appealed, claiming (1) plaintiff was exempt from the provisions of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 151, § 1A because she was a bona fide executive; (2) the judge's method of calculating overtime compensation for plaintiff, a salaried employee, was incorrect; and (3) Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 151, §§ 1A, 1B, were unconstitutionally vague as applied. Plaintiff cross appealed the denial of triple damages. The court reversed the judgment for plaintiff and affirmed the judgment denying punitive damages. The judge correctly concluded that plaintiff was not employed as a bona fide executive at defendant store and thus was not exempt from statutory overtime provisions. Nevertheless, the defendant store did not violate Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 151, § 1A. Plaintiff's cross appeal was moot.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment for plaintiff and affirmed the judgment denying punitive damages. Although plaintiff was not employed as a bona fide executive at defendant store and thus was not exempt from statutory overtime provisions, the defendant store did not violate overtime statute.

Commonwealth v. Lowder


SJC-08124


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


432 Mass. 92; 731 N.E.2d 510; 2000 Mass. LEXIS 378


May 4, 2000, Argued  
July 10, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Bristol. Civil action commenced in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk on May 6, 1999. The case was heard by Lynch, J.



DISPOSITION: Order of the single justice affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commonwealth challenged the judgment of the Supreme Judicial Court for the County of Suffolk (Massachusetts) which denied appellant's petition, filed pursuant to Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 211, § 3, for a declaration that a trial judge lacked the authority to enter a required finding of not guilty until appellant rested its case, and for an order reinstating the prosecution in a criminal narcotics case.


OVERVIEW: After a jury was empanelled in a narcotics prosecution, the prosecutor delivered his opening statement. Thereafter, at recess, the trial judge announced the opening statement did not state a case sufficient to be presented to a jury. Ignoring the prosecutor's request to be heard on the matter, the trial judge entered verdicts of not guilty on both indictments. The trial court denied appellant Commonwealth's petition, filed pursuant to Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 211, § 3, for a declaration that the trial judge lacked the authority to enter a finding of not guilty and for an order reinstating the prosecution. The court concluded that although judges had the power to enter findings of not guilty at the early stages of trial, the trial judge abused his discretion by exercising it as he did in the narcotics prosecution. However, the court also concluded that double jeopardy principles barred appellant from trying defendant anew for the offenses of which the trial judge acquitted him.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order because, although the trial judge abused his discretion by exercising it as he did in the narcotics prosecution, double jeopardy principles barred appellant Commonwealth from trying defendant anew for the offenses of which the trial judge acquitted him.

 

279 of 756 DOCUMENTS


Rotkiewicz v. Sadowsky


SJC-08117


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


431 Mass. 748; 730 N.E.2d 282; 2000 Mass. LEXIS 356


February 11, 2000, Argued  
June 20, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Franklin. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on December 19, 1991. The case was tried before Francis X. Spina, J., and a motion for a new trial was heard by him. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant private citizen appealed from a Franklin Superior Court Department (Massachusetts) jury verdict favoring plaintiff police officer in his defamation and intentional infliction of emotional distress lawsuit.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff police officer filed suit against defendant private citizen and alleged that defendant defamed him in, and intentionally caused him emotional distress via, various statements that plaintiff had committed improprieties. Trial court jury found for plaintiff, although it did so without having been instructed on the actual malice standard for public officials. Defendant appealed and raised as error the lack of an actual malice jury instruction. The court reversed and remanded for a new trial. The court opined that pre-instruction objection was sufficient to preserve claim of improper failure to instruct on actual malice. The court also found that police officers were public officials, given the need for robust debate on police actions, and that as a result, plaintiff was required to prove actual malice (defined as knowledge that an accusation was false or reckless disregard of its falsity). Finally, the court noted that actual malice was also required for emotional distress claim. Accordingly, the verdict was vacated and the case remanded for a retrial.


OUTCOME: Judgment favoring plaintiff in a defamation and intentional infliction of emotional distress suit was reversed, and the case was remanded for retrial. The court noted that plaintiff was a public official, and thus trial court was required to instruct jurors that to recover plaintiff had to establish actual malice, that is, that defendant knew his accusations were false or recklessly disregarded the chance that they were false.

 

280 of 756 DOCUMENTS


Commissioner of Revenue v. J.C. Penney Co.


SJC-08186


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


431 Mass. 684; 730 N.E.2d 266; 2000 Mass. LEXIS 350


March 9, 2000, Argued  
June 14, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected October 6, 2000.


PRIOR HISTORY: Suffolk. Appeal from a decision of the Appellate Tax Board. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.



DISPOSITION: Decision of the Appellate Tax Board reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Commissioner of Revenue appealed decision of Appellate Tax Board, Suffolk (Massachusetts), ordering abatement of use tax assessed on catalogs defendant taxpayer mailed from out-of-state locations to Massachusetts residents, arguing taxpayer's catalog distribution was taxable "use" of property under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 64I, § 1.


OVERVIEW: Defendant taxpayer, an out-of-state corporation engaged in retail merchandising, mailed three catalogs each year to Massachusetts residents. Plaintiff Commissioner of Revenue assessed a use tax on the catalogs, which defendant paid and then applied for an abatement, which was denied. Defendant appealed to the Appellate Tax Board. The board ordered an abatement, holding mailing the catalogs from out-of-state locations to state residents was not a taxable "use" of tangible personal property items in the state as required by Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 64I, §1. Plaintiff appealed. The appellate court reversed, holding the assessment of a use tax on catalogs and other promotional materials that a Massachusetts merchant sends to residents of the Commonwealth by interstate mail was consistent with the plain language of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 64I, § 2, and its underlying policy, and defendant taxpayer had not met its burden of proving it was entitled to abatement.


OUTCOME: Appellate Tax Board decision was reversed, as court concluded "use" encompassed commercial utilization of direct mail catalogs to conduct business in Massachusetts markets, so use tax should be assessed on such materials sent state residents by interstate mail, and defendant taxpayer had not met burden of proving entitlement to abatement.

Shea v. Boston Edison Co.


SJC-08160


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


431 Mass. 251; 727 N.E.2d 41; 2000 Mass. LEXIS 173


March 6, 2000, Argued  
April 19, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk on March 2, 1998. The case was reported by Lynch, J.



DISPOSITION: Remanded to the county court where a judgment is to be entered declaring that the contested charges are not unconstitutional in any of the aspects raised by the plaintiffs, and that, as a consequence, the plaintiffs are not entitled to the relief they seek.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs and defendants requested a determination whether Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 25 § 19, 20 (1997) charges were constitutional under Mass. Const. Part II, ch. 1, § 1, art. 4, in plaintiffs', electric customers, declaratory relief suit against defendants, utility companies, in the Supreme Judicial Court for Suffolk County (Massachusetts).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff customers of investor-owned utilities (IOUs) sought declaratory and other relief against defendant IOUs and state organizations that charges under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 25 § 19, 20 (1997) were unconstitutional under Mass. Const. Part II, ch. 1, § 1, art. 4, and U.S. Const. amend. XIV equal protection. The charges were assessed only against IOU customers. The charge proceeds were used to fund energy efficiency activities, to develop and promote energy efficiency projects, and to reduce pollution and convert waste to energy. Remanding for further proceedings, the court determined the charges were constitutional excise taxes and not regulatory fees, because the charges compensated a government entity to provide services for the general public and were not paid by choice. The charges were constitutional because they were reasonable and benefitted all state citizens even though in different manners and disproportionately, and were rationally related to legitimate state interests.


OUTCOME: The court remanded, finding the statutory charges were excise taxes and not regulatory fees, because the charges compensated a governmental entity to provide services for the general public and were not paid by choice; and were constitutional, because the charges were reasonable and benefitted all citizens even though disproportionately.

Commonwealth v. Williams


SJC-08154


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


431 Mass. 71; 725 N.E.2d 217; 2000 Mass. LEXIS 114


February 8, 2000, Argued  
March 16, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Hampden. Indictment found and returned in the Superior Court Department on August 7, 1997. A motion to dismiss was heard by Francis R. Fecteau, J. The Supreme Judicial Court on its own initiative transferred the case from the Appeals Court.



DISPOSITION: The order dismissing indictment no. 97-1649 vacated. Case remanded to the Superior Court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The plaintiff Commonwealth appealed from an order in a Massachusetts Superior Court which dismissed a second indictment against the defendant for unlawful possession of marijuana with intent to distribute on the ground of collateral estoppel.


OVERVIEW: The lower court dismissed an indictment charging defendant, as a second or subsequent offender, with unlawful possession of marijuana with intent to distribute, a violation of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 94C, § 32C (b). The judge ordered dismissal on the ground of collateral estoppel because a previous indictment for the same offense had been dismissed on motion of the plaintiff Commonwealth, after the defendant's motion to suppress the evidence had been granted, and the Commonwealth had not pursued an interlocutory appeal. The Commonwealth appealed arguing that the judge relied on the doctrine of collateral estoppel as an incident of due process protection, and the court declines to apply collateral estoppel as a corollary of due process against the Commonwealth through U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court affirmed because the Commonwealth, in choosing not to exercise an appeal from the suppression order, was bound under issue preclusion principles by it.


OUTCOME: Because the Commonwealth, in choosing not to exercise an appeal from suppression order, was bound under issue preclusion principles by the suppression order, the court affirmed the order to dismiss the indictment on the ground of collateral estoppel.

 

283 of 756 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Medina


SJC-07936


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


430 Mass. 800; 723 N.E.2d 986; 2000 Mass. LEXIS 97


December 9, 1999, Argued  
February 17, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended March 20, 2000.


PRIOR HISTORY: Suffolk. Indictment found and returned in the Superior Court on April 10, 1975. Following review by this court, 380 Mass. 565, 404 N.E.2d 1228 (1980), a motion for a new trial, filed on July 10, 1996, was heard by Vieri Volterra, J. A request for leave to appeal was considered by Marshall, J.



DISPOSITION: Order denying motion for a new trial affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged order of Suffolk Superior Court (Massachusetts), which denied his motion for a new trial after he was convicted of murder in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant moved for a new trial, arguing that subsequent court decisions regarding burden-shifting presumptions in jury instructions rendered the trial court's malice instruction erroneous and prejudicial. The trial court denied the motion. The malice instruction shifted the burden of disproving malice to defendant because it required the presumption of malice if the jury found particular facts and the jury would have understood the instruction to limit their consideration to evidence that bore on the circumstances of the killing and to preclude their consideration of any further evidence of defendant's malice. In light of the evidence regarding the nature of the assault on the victim and her resulting injuries, the jury's finding the predicate fact of an unlawful killing was the functional equivalent of their finding the ultimate fact to be presumed, malice. Defendant was not prejudiced because the evidence did not raise a reasonable doubt as to the killer's malice.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed because although malice instruction shifted burden of disproving malice to defendant, error was harmless beyond reasonable doubt since nature of assault and victim's injuries did not raise reasonable doubt as to killer's malice.

Mass. Bay Transp. Auth. v. Auditor of the Commonwealth


SJC-08014


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


430 Mass. 783; 724 N.E.2d 288; 2000 Mass. LEXIS 96


December 7, 1999, Argued  
February 16, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on December 19, 1996. The case was heard by Margaret R. Hinkle, J., on motions for summary judgment and judgment on the pleadings. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Massachusetts Bay Transportation Authority (MBTA) appealed from a decision by the Superior Court (Massachusetts), allowing appellee Auditor of the Commonwealth's motion for judgment on the pleadings.


OVERVIEW: Appellant decided to hire a contractor to install advertising on its bus shelters, and to clean and maintain the shelters. Because the contract would shift work done by appellant's employees to a private contractor, the contract was required to comply with the privatization law, Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 7, §§ 52-55. Appellee objected to the contract. The lower court granted appellee judgment on the pleadings. The Massachusetts Supreme Court affirmed, finding that there was ample evidence that appellant did not clearly establish that its "in-house" and "contract" cost estimates were based on the same number of bus shelters. Further, the contractor's predecessor-in-interest did not clearly establish that it was in "good standing" with relevant state and federal taxing authorities. Appellee's objections, therefore, were reasonable and followed the statutory mandate that he independently review the contract. The court also determined appellant lacked standing to challenge a state law on constitutional grounds.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed. Appellee's objections were grounded in the record and well within his statutory powers. Appellant, a creation of the state, lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of a state statute.

Commonwealth v. Crawford


SJC-07770


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


430 Mass. 683; 722 N.E.2d 960; 2000 Mass. LEXIS 19


November 4, 1999, Argued  
January 28, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Indictments found and returned in the Superior Court Department on July 19, 1990. Following review by this court, 417 Mass. 358 (1994), a motion to correct sentences, filed on October 24, 1996, was heard by Vieri Volterra, J., and additional postconviction motions, filed on January 7, 1998, were heard by Elizabeth B. Donovan, J. The Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.

As Amended February 18, 2000.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, convicted of involuntary manslaughter of his girl friend and her unborn fetus, appealed from the denial of his motion to correct sentences and additional postconviction motions in the Superior Court Department, Suffolk (Massachusetts); the Supreme Judicial Court granted an application for direct appellate review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, convicted for the involuntary manslaughter of his girl friend and her unborn fetus, filed a post-appeal motion to correct sentences which was denied. He moved to amend his post-appeal motion, renewing his double jeopardy claims and raising new issues. This motion was denied and the new issues deemed waived. Defendant appealed. The court affirmed, holding that for crimes of violence against multiple victims the appropriate "unit of prosecution" is the person assaulted or killed, not the underlying criminal act, and that the judicial decision to extend criminal common-law protections to viable fetuses did not offend double jeopardy. Regarding the "new" issues of the amended motion, vagueness of the term viability, viability jury instruction, and defendant's knowledge of viability, the court held these issues were sufficiently developed at the time of the defendant's trial to alert him that viability was a live issue, and trial court properly deemed them waived.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of defendant's motions for postconviction relief, holding that defendant's separate sentences for killing both the victim and her viable fetus did not violate double jeopardy principles, and that defendant's issues relating to viability were properly deemed waived.

Opinion of the Justices to the Senate


SJC-08145


SUPREME JUDICIAL COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


430 Mass. 1205; 723 N.E.2d 1; 2000 Mass. LEXIS 17



January 24, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended February 18, 2000.



DISPOSITION: The answer to the question is, "No."


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Senate submitted a question to the court (Massachusetts) requesting the court's opinion on whether a newly proposed bill that restricted access to buffer zones outside reproductive health care facilities violated the constitutional right of freedom of speech or the constitutional right of the people peaceably to assemble.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner Senate was concerned about the constitutionality of a proposed bill that created fixed buffer zones of 25 feet from any portion of an entrance, exit, or driveway of a reproductive health care facility, as well as a fixed corridor zone from a clinic entrance to the street. With limited exceptions, the proposed bill prohibited anyone from knowingly entering into or remaining within the buffer zones. The court found a "solemn occasion" existed, which made it proper for it to answer the question regarding constitutionality. The court concluded the proposed bill was a content-neutral statute that was narrowly tailored to serve a substantial government interest. The bill left open ample alternative means of communication. Demonstrators could still protest as they previously had done, but were simply constrained to do so outside the buffer zone. Further, the bill would not violate the right to assemble peaceably under U.S. Const. amend. I.


OUTCOME: Proposed bill did not violate constitutional rights of freedom of speech or freedom to assemble. Restricting protesters to a 25-foot buffer zone outside reproductive health care facilities did not discriminate on viewpoint and left open ample alternative channels of communication.

Cristo v. Evangelidis


No. 15-P-91.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS

A motion for summary judgment was heard by David Ricciardone, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Where plaintiff filed a § 1983 action alleging defendant sheriff terminated his employment in retaliation for his exercising his First Amendment rights, the trial court erred in denying defendant's motion for summary judgment based on the defense of qualified immunity; plaintiff's speech, while related to matters of public concern, was undertaken in his capacity as an employee of the sheriff's office and not as a private citizen, since there was no disputed fact issue that his communications were made exclusively to fulfill his responsibilities as the director of payroll and human resources.


OUTCOME: The order was vacated.

HOLDINGS: [1]-Police had probable cause to arrest him, as details of his location, ethnicity, clothing, and means of transportation provided by a confidential informant (CI) allowed the inference that the CI's knowledge was based on personal observation; [2]-His veracity was satisfied by the accuracy of his prior tips, police corroboration of the details, and their knowledge of his identity and location; [3]-The court properly found that the CI's reliability was established by his prior tip resulting in an arrest and seizure of contraband; though evidence obtained from that tip was suppressed, this occurred after officers in the instant case relied on his tip, and there was no indication the reasons for suppression impugned his accuracy or credibility.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

United States v. Occhiuto, 784 F.3d 862, 2015 U.S. App. LEXIS 7368 (1st Cir. Mass., 2015)

Commonwealth v. Rezendes


No. 14-P-753.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


88 Mass. App. Ct. 369; 37 N.E.3d 672; 2015 Mass. App. LEXIS 149


May 11, 2015, Argued 
September 17, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Rezendes, 2015 Mass. LEXIS 880 (Mass., Dec. 3, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Plymouth. Indictments found and returned in the Superior Court Department on February 18, 2011.

The cases were tried before Merita A. Hopkins, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The term "deadly weapon" in the context of the Massachusetts Armed Career Criminal Act (MACCA), Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 269, § 10G, was distinct from the term "dangerous weapon" as applied in Massachusetts common law; [2]-For the purposes of conviction under ch. 269, § 10G, a deadly weapon was a weapon that was inherently deadly; [3]-To interpret the term "deadly weapon" as interchangeable with the term "dangerous weapon" in this context would be inconsistent with the ordinary usage of the terms, the intent of the Massachusetts legislature, and the language of the federal analog, on which the MACCA was modeled; [4]-A pen was not a deadly weapon under the MACCA and defendant's juvenile adjudication for assault and battery by means of a dangerous weapon could not have formed the basis for a conviction under the MACCA.


OUTCOME: Conviction as armed career criminal reversed. Matter remanded for resentencing. Remaining judgments affirmed.

Chiulli v. Liberty Mutual Insurance, Inc.


No. 14-P-430.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


87 Mass. App. Ct. 229; 28 N.E.3d 482; 2015 Mass. App. LEXIS 33


October 1, 2014, Argued 
April 2, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on March 18, 2013.

A special motion to dismiss was heard by Frances A. McIntyre, J.
Chiulli v. Newbury Fine Dining, Inc., 895 F. Supp. 2d 277, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 143888 (D. Mass., 2012)



DISPOSITION: Order denying special motion to dismiss affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Defendant insurer was not entitled to the protections of the anti-Strategic Lawsuit Against Public Participation (SLAPP) statute, Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 231, § 59H, because plaintiff's claims were based on the insurer's failure to effectuate prompt, fair, and equitable settlement of plaintiff's claims and not solely on the insurer's petitioning activities; [2]-To permit an insurer to use an anti-SLAPP defense to defeat Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 176D actions whenever an insurer had opted to try the underlying tort action would effectively gut Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 176D; [3]-The insurer did not have standing to bring a special motion to dismiss under the anti-SLAPP statute because there was no proof of direct action by the insurer with regard to the underlying trial and something more than a mere contractual connection to petitioning activity was required to establish standing.


OUTCOME: Order denying special motion to dismiss affirmed.

An application for leave to prosecute an interlocutory appeal was allowed by Margot Botsford, J., in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk, and the appeal was reported by her to the Appeals Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A trial court's finding that a trooper agreed that a photograph of defendant's license plate accurately depicted the license plate and its cover as they appeared when defendant was stopped was not clearly erroneous as the trooper testified that the photograph looked like the vehicle that he saw on that particular day and that he believed the photograph showed the actual car and the license plate on the vehicle on the day of the stop; [2]-The finding that the photograph showed a clear license plate was not clearly erroneous because the judge did not use clear as an antonym for tinted, but to mean that the cover did not impede the visibility of the license plate, regardless of any tint; the appellate court reviewed the photograph and agreed; [3]-A blanket regulatory ban on license plate covers could not be reconciled with the plain language of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 90, § 6.


OUTCOME: Order allowing motion to suppress affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Almeida


No. 11-P-1745.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


83 Mass. App. Ct. 451; 985 N.E.2d 402; 2013 Mass. App. LEXIS 49


January 16, 2013, Argued 
March 27, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by Commonwealth v. Almeida, 465 Mass. 1108, 990 N.E.2d 562, 2013 Mass. LEXIS 590 (2013)
Superseded by, Remanded by Commonwealth v. Almeida, 2014 Mass. LEXIS 136 (Mass., Mar. 21, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Bristol. Petition for civil commitment filed in the Superior Court Department on July 15, 2009. The case was heard by Robert C. Cosgrove, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment by the Bristol Superior Court Department (Massachusetts) that civil confined him as a sexually dangerous person pursuant to Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 123A; defendant claimed that he did not satisfy the statutory requirements, or, if he did, the statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: In 1996, defendant repeatedly targeted a 17-year old for his sexual attentions and subsequent intimidation. In 1999, he used force against a police officer in conjunction with his exhibitionistic act. In the governing offense, defendant entered the victim's apartment and approached her in her bathroom while she was showering, thereby putting her in fear. The trial judge concluded that defendant suffered from a mental abnormality that manifested itself, among other ways, in voyeuristic and exhibitionistic behavior. The judge also found that defendant was likely to reoffend. The appellate court found, inter alia, that as defendant was likely to act on his voyeuristic impulses and exhibitionistic compulsions in the future, he was a menace to the health and safety of others. The significant procedural and substantive requirements in ch. 123A rendered the civil commitment of a menacing sexual offender consistent with the concept of ordered liberty. Accordingly, as defendant's predicted sexual offenses and associated conduct would instill in the victim apprehension of being subjected to a contact sex crime, he was properly committed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

313 of 756 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Nee


No. 11-P-1952.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


83 Mass. App. Ct. 441; 985 N.E.2d 118; 2013 Mass. App. LEXIS 47


February 5, 2013, Argued 
March 19, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Nee, 465 Mass. 1106, 989 N.E.2d 899, 2013 Mass. LEXIS 628 (Mass., June 6, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Suffolk. Complaint received and sworn to in the South Boston Division of the Boston Municipal Court Department on November 15, 2010. After transfer to the Central Division of the Boston Municipal Court Department, the case was tried before Eleanor C. Sinnott, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury for the Central Division of the Boston Municipal Court Department, Suffolk County, Massachusetts, found defendant guilty of affray as defined in Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 277, § 39. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: A friend of the victim observed defendant in a group of three or four youths kicking the victim as he lay on the ground and rising above the victim's body to strike him with a fist. The friend was sufficiently alarmed to tackle defendant, wrestle him to the ground, and hold him until the police arrived notwithstanding punches and kicks directed at him by others in defendant's group. Thus, the evidence sufficed to establish that defendant was one of two or more persons fighting together in a public place, to the terror of persons lawfully there. Also, defendant, the youth in the white sweatshirt, the tall youth with dark skin, and 10 or more others formed together to attack the victim and two of his friends who were lawfully present in a public park. Further, nothing in the legislative definition of affray required the fighting together of two or more person be by agreement or design of all the participants. Thus, because the victim and his two friends were lawfully in the public park, and the attack on them by defendant and his group satisfied the "fighting together of two or more persons" element, the evidence was sufficient to convict defendant of the offense of affray.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

Commonwealth v. Jones

APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS

November 9, 2012, Argued 
February 15, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Jones, 465 Mass. 1103, 987 N.E.2d 595, 2013 Mass. LEXIS 397 (Mass., May 3, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Plymouth. Complaint received and sworn to in the Brockton Division of the District Court Department on June 22, 2009. A pretrial motion to suppress evidence was heard by Julie J. Bernard, J., and the case was heard by Paul J. McCallum, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the denial of defendant's suppression motion by the Brockton Division of the Plymouth District Court Department, Massachusetts, defendant appealed his conviction of carrying a firearm without a license under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 269, § 10(a) and possession of a firearm without a firearm identification card (FID card) under § 10(h)(1). He was also convicted of drinking alcohol in public.


OVERVIEW: The police stopped defendant with a reasonable suspicion of public consumption of alcohol, but had no subjective intent to arrest him and did not fear for their own safety. The appellate court held that once defendant admitted that he was carrying an object potentially harmful to the inquiring officer, there was an objective basis for a patfrisk under the Fourth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. IV, and Mass. Const. Decl. Rights art. XIV. Further, the encounter occurred in an area with a history of firearms violations and defendant was unfamiliar to the officers and was intoxicated. Gomes was distinguished due to the question and answer as to whether defendant was carrying any harmful objects. Defendant's Second Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. II, rights were not violated by the firearm license or FID card requirements as legislation reasonably regulating possession and use of firearms as an exercise of the police power to serve the public health, safety, and welfare was permitted. Defendant lacked standing to challenge the licensure statute as he had not applied for a license or an FID card. The lack of a license or an FID card was an affirmative defense to be proved by defendant.


OUTCOME: The judgments were affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Figueroa

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Hampden. Indictment found and returned in the Superior Court Department on December 30, 2008. The case was tried before C. Brian McDonald, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed her conviction by the Hampden Superior Court Department, Massachusetts, of reckless endangerment of a child in violation of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 265, § 13L, and sentence to a two-year term of probation.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that there was sufficient evidence to support the conviction. The six-month-old child, his 15-year-old mother, and his father lived with defendant, the child's grandmother. Defendant refused to take the mother and the child to the hospital, and confiscated the mother's cell phone to prevent her from summoning an ambulance. Defendant had a duty to act. Delaying or obstructing medical care for the child created a substantial and unjustifiable risk of serious bodily injury. The jury instruction on defendant's duty to act was erroneous, but did not require reversal. The jury was properly instructed that the mother's custody of the child did not absolve defendant of liability. The Commonwealth did not have to prove that medical care would have alleviated a risk. A Ciampa instruction was not required. The notations as to the Department of Social Services's prior involvement in the medical records were not unduly prejudicial.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Norfolk. Indictments found and returned in the Superior Court Department on May 21, 2008. A pretrial motion to suppress evidence was heard by Kenneth J. Fishman, J., and the cases were tried before Wendie I. Gershengorn, J.
Commonwealth v. Cantelli, 2009 Mass. Super. LEXIS 113 (Mass. Super. Ct., 2009)



DISPOSITION: Order denying motion to suppress affirmed. Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following the denial of his motion to suppress, defendant was convicted by the Norfolk Superior Court Department, Massachusetts, of possession of an infernal machine (explosive device) under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 266, § 102A and two counts of improper storage of a firearm under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 140, § 131L(a) and 131L(b). Defendant appealed. He was acquitted of two other counts of improper storage of a firearm.

OUTCOME: The denial of the motion to suppress was affirmed. The judgments were affirmed.

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Plymouth. Indictment found and returned in the Superior Court Department on June 1, 2007. The case was tried before Wendie I. Gershengorn, J.
Commonwealth v. Sullivan, 457 Mass. 1103, 928 N.E.2d 949, 2010 Mass. LEXIS 535 (2010)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for possession of child pornography by the Superior Court Department (Massachusetts). He argued, inter alia, that the photograph of a naked adolescent girl that he printed from a computer at a public library did not contain a lewd exhibition of the pubic area of the girl or of her breasts as required by Mass. OVERVIEW: A librarian found defendant printing out a photograph of a naked girl on a beach. After the library closed, the librarian reviewed the computer's Web site history and found other photographs of children on beaches and in other settings, with and without clothes. OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Doe v. Sex Offender Registry Bd.


No. 10-P-435.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


81 Mass. App. Ct. 639; 966 N.E.2d 826; 2012 Mass. App. LEXIS 177


February 9, 2011, Argued 
April 27, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Essex. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on January 2, 2008. The case was heard by Richard E. Welch, III, J., on motions for judgment on the pleadings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a 10-year-old boy (the minor), appealed from a Superior Court Department (Massachusetts) judgment affirming his classification as a level two sex offender which required him to, inter alia, register annually with the Sex Offender Registry Board (SORB) and with the police department pursuant to Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 6, § 178K(2)(b). The minor argued, inter alia, that evidence of his history was unreliable.


OVERVIEW: The SORB hearing examiner's decision relied heavily on the contents of the evaluation prepared by the Juvenile Court clinic which contained an extensive factual history regarding the minor and his family, much of it based on layered hearsay. The minor contended that his history should not have been used as a basis for his classification. Whatever basis existed for the SORB's determination that 803 Mass. Code Regs. § 1.40(9)(c)(2) generally enhanced the risk of reoffending, there was no explanation. Each of the five factors used sprung from a conclusion that an offender's choice of a victim's age, sex, or location indicated a greater risk of deviancy and increased the likelihood of reoffense. But a sound application of those factors to derive a true and accurate assessment of an offender's potential for reoffending had to take into account the offender's age. There was no question that the minor was a troubled youngster deeply in need of the services he was receiving. But the classification decision rested on unreliable hearsay and the application without explanation of predictive criteria that did not on their face take account of sexual activity between prepubescent children.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed. The case was remanded for entry of a judgment vacating the SORB's classification order.

Commonwealth v. Colon


No. 10-P-23.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


81 Mass. App. Ct. 8; 958 N.E.2d 56; 2011 Mass. App. LEXIS 1483


February 8, 2011, Argued 
December 6, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Suffolk. Indictments found and returned in the Superior Court Department on August 3, 2004. The cases were tried before Patrick F. Brady, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Suffolk Superior Court Department (Massachusetts) convicted defendant of assault and battery by means of a dangerous weapon, carrying a firearm without a license, and possession of ammunition without a firearm identification card. In a second trial, another jury convicted defendant of being an armed career criminal based on three predicate offenses, Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 269, § 10G(c) as a sentence enhancement. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that a certified record of conviction was not sufficient evidence that he was convicted of a predicate "violent crime" under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 140, § 121, for purposes of the armed career criminal sentencing enhancements of § 10G(a)-(c). The appellate court held that a certified record was sufficient for purposes when the predicate offense was assault and battery on a police officer (ABPO), Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 265, § 13D, but not when the predicate offense was assault and battery, Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 265, § 13A(a). The Commonwealth presented no extrinsic evidence to indicate whether the convictions were based on harmful, reckless, or offensive battery. Nor did the Commonwealth present evidence of the underlying facts of defendant's convictions. There was insufficient proof of a harmful or reckless battery conviction, but sufficient proof on the lesser included offense of armed career criminal status based on the two ABPO convictions as predicate offenses. Defendant, having never challenged that his 2003 assault and battery conviction on direct appeal or pursuant to Mass.R.Crim.P. 30(b), was not allowed to collaterally attack it here.


OUTCOME: The judgment on the armed career criminal enhancement on three predicate violent crimes was reversed, the verdict was set aside insofar as it found that defendant had committed three prior violent crimes, and the matter was remanded for resentencing on the lesser included offense of an armed career criminal enhancement based on two predicate violent crimes. The remaining judgments were affirmed.

February 14, 2011, Argued 
October 31, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Bd. of Managers of Old Colony Vill. Condo. v. Preu, 2012 Mass. LEXIS 334 (Mass., Mar. 30, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Barnstable. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on May 6, 2009. The case was heard by Regina L. Quinlan, J., and a request for assessment of attorney's fees and costs was considered by her.
Bd. of Managers of the Old Colony Vill. Condo. v. Preu, 2009 Mass. Super. LEXIS 417 (Mass. Super. Ct., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff condominium board appealed a judgment by the Barnstable Superior Court Department (Massachusetts) that defendant unit owner's communication by signs and posters critical of management in the condominium trash room was pure speech that was protected by the First Amendment.

OUTCOME: The judgment was vacated as to costs, and otherwise affirmed; the matter was remanded for further proceedings and the parties' requests for appellate attorney's fees were denied.

Commonwealth v. DeWeldon


No. 10-P-1357.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


80 Mass. App. Ct. 626; 954 N.E.2d 1140; 2011 Mass. App. LEXIS 1278


May 4, 2011, Argued 
October 12, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Commonwealth v. DeWeldon, 461 Mass. 1103, 958 N.E.2d 530, 2011 Mass. LEXIS 1130 (Mass., Dec. 1, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Barnstable. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on December 17, 2007. A motion to dismiss was heard by Richard F. Connon, J., and a motion for reconsideration was considered by him; the case was heard by Gary A. Nickerson, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment and order of commitment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a civil trial, the Superior Court Department (Massachusetts) found defendant prisoner to be a sexually dangerous person (SDP) as defined in Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 123A, § 1, and committed him to a treatment center for one day to life pursuant to Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 123A, § 14(d). On appeal, the prisoner argued that the judge should have dismissed the petition because he was not a lawful prisoner at the time of the filing of the petition.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner had previously pled guilty to indecent assault and battery on a child under age fourteen in Massachusetts and second degree child molestation in Rhode Island. The prisoner was later found by a Rhode Island judge to be in violation of probation. His original Rhode Island sentence was imposed. Upon parole, his Massachusetts sentence was imposed. The appellate court held that it could not assume the Department of Correction (DOC) would mechanically adopt the determination by a separate government of the quality of rehabilitation or the quantity of credit attributable to those programs. In the proper exercise of the police power, DOC retained separate sovereign authority and nondelegable responsibility to assess the entitlement and the timing of the release of a recidivist offender into its own population. As of the date of the Commonwealth's submission of a petition under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 123A, § 12(b), DOC had not exercised its discretionary judgment of his entitlement to sentence credit. That determination was necessary for an entitlement to release before the petition date. He therefore remained a lawful prisoner subject to the process of the SDP statutory scheme.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Cent. Water Dist. Assocs. v. Cedar Meadow Lake Watershed Dist.


No. 10-P-773.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


80 Mass. App. Ct. 468; 954 N.E.2d 38; 2011 Mass. App. LEXIS 1208


February 10, 2011, Argued 
September 26, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Worcester. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on July 25, 1997. The case was tried before John S. McCann, J.; a motion for summary judgment on the issue of the interest award was heard by him, and entry of final judgment was ordered by him.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant watershed district took plaintiff owner's land by eminent domain; plaintiff filed suit under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 79, § 14, for an assessment of damages. After a jury assessed damages, defendant sought a determination that the statutory interest rate should be applied to the award. The Worcester Superior Court (Massachusetts) applied that rate and denied plaintiff's request for compound interest. Plaintiff appealed.


OVERVIEW: The jury entered its award over 12 years after the taking occurred. Plaintiff argued that the rate and method of calculating interest set by Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 79, § 37, did not provided it with just and reasonable compensation as mandated by U.S. Const. amend. V and Mass. Const. Decl. Rights art. X. The appellate court held that as plaintiff failed to establish that the rate of interest set by ch. 79, § 37, fell substantially below the standard of constitutional reasonableness, it failed to rebut the presumption that the statutory rate met constitutional requirements. By offering only a single comparator, rather than a range or composite of interest rates based on different types of low-risk securities, plaintiff failed to adequately identify what constituted a constitutionally reasonable interest rate. However, given the extended delay between the time of the taking and the time of payment, the compounding of interest was necessary to place plaintiff in as good a position pecuniarily as it would have occupied if the payment had coincided with the appropriation.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed insofar as it denied plaintiff's request for compound interest on the award. The remainder of the judgment was affirmed.

 

324 of 756 DOCUMENTS


Harvard 45 Assocs., LLC v. Allied Props. & Mortgs., Inc.


No. 10-P-598.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


80 Mass. App. Ct. 203; 952 N.E.2d 411; 2011 Mass. App. LEXIS 1136


January 18, 2011, Argued 
August 25, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Harvard 45 Assocs., LLC v. Allied Props. & Mortgs., 460 Mass. 1115, 957 N.E.2d 241, 2011 Mass. LEXIS 1045 (2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Land Court Department on September 19, 2008. The case was heard by Alexander H. Sands, J., on motions for summary judgment.
Harvard 45 Assocs., LLC v. Allied Props. & Mortgs., 2010 Mass. LCR LEXIS 5 (2010)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff filed suit in the Suffolk Land Court Department (Massachusetts) to quiet title to property on which defendant held a mortgage. Plaintiff alleged the mortgage had been discharged under the "Obsolete Mortgages" statute, Mass. Gen. Laws Ann. ch. 260, § 33, because defendant failed to record an extension with the Registry of Deeds as statutorily required. The land court entered judgment in favor of plaintiff. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant argued that § 33 was ambiguous. However, under the plain language of § 33, the mortgage had been discharged. Section 33 clearly discharged, as matter of law, all mortgages five years after the date on which they became due. The mortgage at issue, clearly within the purview of the statute, expired on August 31, 2001, and was therefore discharged by operation of law on August 31, 2006. The extension agreement did not, contrary to defendant's assertion, extend the maturity date of the mortgage for purposes of § 33 because the extension agreement was never recorded and was therefore not a valid extension under the clear requirements of § 33. Even if the agreement had been recorded, it failed to satisfy the requirements of Mass. Gen. Laws Ann. ch. 260, § 34. Despite defendant's argument that plaintiff lacked good title because the execution which plaintiff acquired the property was dissolved pursuant to Mass. Gen. Laws Ann. ch. 223, § 75, the statute was not applicable because § 75 and Mass. Gen. Laws Ann. ch. 223, § 74 concerned attachment of personal property and plaintiff acquired title to the real property following a sheriff's sale.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

325 of 756 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Moran


No. 10-P-231.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


80 Mass. App. Ct. 8; 951 N.E.2d 356; 2011 Mass. App. LEXIS 1083


October 13, 2010, Argued 
August 4, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Commonwealth v. Moran, 460 Mass. 1114, 954 N.E.2d 539, 2011 Mass. LEXIS 851 (Mass., Sept. 28, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Norfolk. Complaint received and sworn to in the Brookline Division of the District Court Department on July 20, 2009. The case was heard by Patricia G. Curtin, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following a bench trial, the Brookline Division of the District Court Department, Norfolk (Massachusetts) convicted defendant of accosting or annoying a person of the opposite sex in violation of Mass. Gen. Laws Ann. ch. 272, § 53. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the evidence was insufficient to support his conviction, because his activity was neither offensive nor disorderly, and the evidence only reveled a single act but, given the statutory prohibition of "acts" that accost or annoy, conviction required proof of at least two. The appellate court disagreed, concluding that the evidence was sufficient to prove beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant's conduct was offensive and disorderly. Within reaching distance of the victim, defendant drew attention to himself, grabbed the genital area of his pants, and moved his hand up and down to mimic masturbation, activity a fact finder could conclude was repugnant to the prevailing sent of what is decent or moral. The appellate court also concluded that, contrary to defendant's claim, Mass. Gen. Laws Ann. ch. 272, § 53, was not restricted to coverage of situations where a defendant engaged in more than one act, a limitation that would lead to absurd results.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

326 of 756 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Patterson


No. 09-P-2346.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


79 Mass. App. Ct. 316; 946 N.E.2d 130; 2011 Mass. App. LEXIS 595


October 27, 2010, Argued 
April 25, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Patterson, 460 Mass. 1106, 950 N.E.2d 438, 2011 Mass. LEXIS 638 (Mass., June 29, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Norfolk. Complaint received and sworn to in the Wrentham Division of the District Court Department on January 28, 2009.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Wrentham Division of the District Court Department (Massachusetts) convicted defendant of improper storage of a firearm, Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 140, § 131L(a). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that § 131L(a) violated the Second Amendment. The appellate court disagreed. The statute did not, on its face, unconstitutionally interfere with an individual's ability to rely on a gun for self-defense, and nothing suggested that its requirements interfered with defendant's right to self-defense. The Commonwealth met its burden of proving that the gun was not under defendant's control. The gun was stored in the pocket of a jacket hanging in an upstairs closet. Defendant was downstairs. The gun was out of its holster, which was lying on the kitchen floor. Children were present, including a boy who at times was closer to the gun than was defendant. The jury had sufficient evidence to find that the gun was not within defendant's control. Defendant argued that the admission of a child's statement violated his right to confrontation under the Sixth Amendment. However, nothing suggested that the statement was made for any purpose other than to secure aid, let alone that the child had in mind that the statement would or could be used to prove some fact at a future criminal trial. The statement, thus, was not testimonial for Sixth Amendment purposes.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

327 of 756 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. McCollum


No. 07-P-1881.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


79 Mass. App. Ct. 239; 945 N.E.2d 937; 2011 Mass. App. LEXIS 546


October 6, 2010, Argued 
April 14, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. McCollum, 460 Mass. 1112, 953 N.E.2d 720, 2011 Mass. LEXIS 945 (2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Suffolk. Indictments found and returned in the Superior Court Department on November 17, 2003. A pretrial motion to suppress evidence was heard by Kenneth J. Fishman, J.; the cases were tried before Margaret R. Hinkle, J., and a motion for a new trial, filed August 7, 2008, was heard by her.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Suffolk Superior Court Department (Massachusetts) of possession of cocaine, unlicensed possession of a firearm, and possession of ammunition without a firearms identification (FID) card. His motion for a new trial was denied, and he appealed his convictions and the denial of his motion.


OVERVIEW: Defendant said insufficient evidence supported his ammunition possession conviction, under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 269, § 10(h). The appellate court disagreed because (1) it showed constructive possession, (2) it showed the ammunition was designed for use in any firearm, and (3) he showed no valid firearms identification card. Evidence of his unlicensed gun possession was sufficient because (1) it showed constructive possession, (2) he showed no license, (3) it showed the gun met the definition in Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 140, § 121, and (4) it showed he and the gun were not found at his home or business. Cocaine possession evidence was insufficient because nothing showed he knew it was present. Admitting a ballistics certificate without live testimony was not harmless as to the gun but was harmless as to the ammunition, because (1) nothing else showed the gun was operable, but (2) the ammunition was identifiable without scientific testing. Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 269, § 10(h) did not violate the Second Amendment because (1) defendant did not live where he and the gun were found, and (2) his criminal history barred compliance with valid licensing rules in Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 140, § 131(d)(i).


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for unlicensed possession of ammunition was affirmed. His cocaine possession conviction was reversed, that verdict was set aside, and judgment entered for defendant on that charge. Defendant's unlawful firearm possession conviction was reversed, that verdict was set aside, and the charge was remanded to the trial court.

Commonwealth v. Miller


No. 10-P-613.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


78 Mass. App. Ct. 860; 944 N.E.2d 179; 2011 Mass. App. LEXIS 291


November 3, 2010, Argued 
March 2, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Commonwealth v. Miller, 460 Mass. 1106, 950 N.E.2d 438, 2011 Mass. LEXIS 606 (Mass., June 29, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Middlesex. Complaint received and sworn to in the Woburn Division of the District Court Department on May 4, 2009. A pretrial motion to suppress evidence was heard by Marianne C. Hinkle, J. An application for leave to prosecute an interlocutory appeal was allowed by Margot Botsford, J., in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk, and the appeal was reported by her to the Appeals Court.



DISPOSITION: Order allowing motion to suppress affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with, inter alia, operating a vehicle under the influence of alcohol, third offense, and failure to properly display registration plates in violation of 540 Mass. Code Regs. 2.23(3) (2008). He filed a motion to suppress, which the trial court allowed. The Commonwealth's application for leave to prosecute an interlocutory appeal was allowed by single justice of the Supreme Judicial Court for Suffolk County, Massachusetts.


OVERVIEW: An officer decided to stop the car defendant was driving because the words "Spirit of America" on its rear license plate were obscured by a black stripe. The stripe did not cover the plate number, the sticker that identified the month of registration, or the word "Massachusetts." The officer did not observe any traffic violation or any erratic operation of any kind. The trial court held that stopping defendant solely for violating § 2.23(3) was illegal because that regulation exceeded the scope of the enabling statute. The appellate court disagreed with this rationale, because evidence obtained after a stop based on an illegal regulation would be admissible. However, § 2.23(3) only related to license plate frames, not "stripes," and thus could not form a basis for the stop. Therefore, the stop was improper and the evidence obtained as a result of that stop had to be suppressed.


OUTCOME: The order allowing the motion to suppress affirmed.

 

329 of 756 DOCUMENTS


Cosgrove v. Hughes


No. 10-P-338.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


78 Mass. App. Ct. 739; 941 N.E.2d 706; 2011 Mass. App. LEXIS 211


November 5, 2010, Argued 
February 15, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Cosgrove v. Hughes, 459 Mass. 1111, 947 N.E.2d 43, 2011 Mass. LEXIS 410 (Mass., Apr. 27, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Suffolk. Civil action commenced in the Suffolk Division of the Probate and Family Court Department on January 6, 2009. The case was heard by E. Chouteau Levine, J., on a motion for summary judgment.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff nieces and nephews of a decedent brought an action against defendant daughter, seeking a declaration that the daughter was not the decedent's child and, thus, that she was not an heir and may not have inherited from his estate. The Suffolk Division of the Probate and Family Court Department (Massachusetts) found that the daughter was the decedent's child and granted her summary judgment motion. The nieces and nephews appealed.


OVERVIEW: The daughter was born before the decedent's marriage. The appellate court found that, although record included evidence indicating that the daughter was not the decedent's child, the father had completed an affidavit in 1944, and based on this affidavit, the daughter's record of birth was filed. The daughter's certified birth certificate stated that her father was the decedent. Once the decedent acknowledged the daughter as his child, under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 190, § 7, she was his child and heir, just as if she had been born in wedlock. That the decedent later did not consistently assert that the daughter was his child did not draw the sufficiency of his acknowledgment itself into question. None of the evidence in the record raised a genuine issue about whether the decedent's signature on the affidavit was knowing and voluntary. The text of § 7 provided no basis for allowing other relatives greater authority to challenge the biological relationship to his or her intestate father of a child born out of wedlock and acknowledged under the statute than would be permitted with respect to a child born during the marriage of that father.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

330 of 756 DOCUMENTS


Santos, petitioner


No. 08-P-536.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


78 Mass. App. Ct. 280; 937 N.E.2d 461; 2010 Mass. App. LEXIS 1478


February 6, 2009, Argued 
November 19, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by Santos v. Commonwealth, 460 Mass. 1101, 949 N.E.2d 923, 2011 Mass. LEXIS 456 (2011)
Superseded by, Remanded by Santos, petitioner, 461 Mass. 565, 962 N.E.2d 726, 2012 Mass. LEXIS 40 (2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Barnstable. Petition filed in the Superior Court Department on June 8, 2005. The case was tried before Linda E. Giles, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to Mass. Gen. Laws Ann. ch. 123A, § 9, petitioner, a convicted sex offender, filed a petition for discharge from commitment as a sexually dangerous person. A jury in the Superior Court Department, Barnstable (Massachusetts) found petitioner remained a sexually dangerous person and he remained civilly committed. Petitioner appealed, arguing the failure to admit his experts' written reports violated his constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: At the hearing on the petition, respondent Commonwealth introduced in evidence the written reports of two qualified examiners (QEs) as psychiatric or psychological records. Over petitioner's objection, the trial judge excluded from evidence the written reports of petitioner's two expert psychologists. The court resolved petitioner's appeal on the basis of statutory construction rather than the constitutional questions. In a prior decision, the court found that a "report" by a non-QE psychiatrist offered in evidence by the Commonwealth was a psychiatric or psychological record within the meaning of Mass. Gen. Laws Ann. ch. 123A, § 14(c), and was therefore admissible just as QE reports were. The provision of § 14(c) was essentially identical to the provision of Mass. Gen. Laws Ann. ch. 123A, § 9. Therefore, § 9 required the admission of petitioner's experts' reports on the same basis as those of the QEs. The error in excluding the written reports of petitioner's experts was prejudicial because only the reports by the QEs were sent with the jury into the jury room, giving them a weight and accessibility during deliberations that petitioner's evidence did not have.


OUTCOME: The judgment was vacated and the case remanded for further proceedings.

 

331 of 756 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Truong


No. 09-P-804.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


78 Mass. App. Ct. 28; 934 N.E.2d 1274; 2010 Mass. App. LEXIS 1298


May 18, 2010, Argued 
October 7, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Essex. Complaint received and sworn to in the Lynn Division of the District Court Department on May 30, 2007. The case was tried before Ellen Flatley, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Lynn Division of the District Court Department (Massachusetts) convicted defendant of possession of ammunition without a firearm identification card, Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 269, § 10(h). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: While testifying, defendant suggested that at the time of the search, he did not know what a shell casing was. In response to this testimony, the Commonwealth was permitted to question defendant about whether he had previously been on probation as a juvenile for possession of ammunition. Defendant argued that this was improper because there was no certified record of the conviction as required by Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 233, § 21. However, the appellate court held that this was not a case where the Commonwealth was trying to impeach defendant by proof of his commission or conviction of a crime and thus Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 233, § 21 did not apply. The method of impeachment by contradiction was proper. The "shell casings" found in defendant's room were "cartridge cases" within the meaning of Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 269, § 10(h). There was no ambiguity which would have invalidated Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 269, § 10(h) under the void-for-vagueness doctrine. Further, the statute bore a reasonable relation to a permissible legislative objective and did not violate due process.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

332 of 756 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Lojko


No. 09-P-24.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


77 Mass. App. Ct. 82; 928 N.E.2d 679; 2010 Mass. App. LEXIS 820


November 9, 2009, Argued 
June 22, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Barnstable. Complaint received and sworn to in the Barnstable Division of the District Court Department on July 17, 2008. The case was heard by Kathryn E. Hand, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed. Finding set aside. Judgment for defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted, following a jury-waived trial, by the Barnstable Division of the District Court Department (Massachusetts) of improper storage of a firearm under Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 140, § 131L(a). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant responded affirmatively when he was asked, during a well-being check based on defendant's suicide threat, that he had a gun in a cooler located outside. Inside the cooler, the officers found a locked box in which there was a firearm. The box was composed of a plastic-type material and measured approximately 10-by-6 inches. The box was designed with a small hole, which lined up with the firearm's trigger guard. The box was found with a cable lock threaded through the hole, thereby securing the firearm in the box as well as locking the box itself. The appellate court held that defendant did not violate § 131L(a) because the statute permitted the storage of a firearm in any place as long as it was secured in a locked container. The word "secured" indicated that the container had to not merely be locked, but securely locked in a way that would deter all but the most persistent from gaining access. The fact that defendant placed the securely locked box in a cooler was of no consequence. Neither the statute nor Massachusetts case law required a gun owner who kept a firearm secured in a locked container to also store that container in a secure location.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed. The finding was set aside. Judgment was entered for defendant.

Roche v. Dir. of the Div. of Marine Fisheries


No. 09-P-447.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


76 Mass. App. Ct. 733; 926 N.E.2d 559; 2010 Mass. App. LEXIS 622


December 8, 2009, Argued 
May 18, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Plymouth. Civil action commenced in the Superior Court Department on May 23, 2006. The case was heard by Richard F. Connon, J., on motions for summary judgment.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff fishermen filed an action against defendant director of the division of marine fisheries, challenging 322 Mass. Code Regs. 7.01(4)(a) (2006), as unconstitutional, and as illegal, arbitrary, and capricious under Mass. Gen. Laws Ann. ch. 30A. The Plymouth Superior Court Department (Massachusetts) ordered that summary judgment enter for the director. The fishermen appealed.


OVERVIEW: Under the challenged regulation, the fishermen were allowed to fish in state waters, but only in accordance with their federal permits, unless they surrendered their federal permits and fished in accordance with their state permits. Prior to the adoption of the regulation, the fishermen avoided federal restrictions by postponing the annual renewal of the federal permits and, in the interim, fishing in state waters under their state permits. Contrary to the fishermen's claims, the state regulation was not preempted by the Magnuson-Stevens Fishery Conservation and Management Act, 16 U.S.C.S. § 1801 et seq., which expressly provided for the state's regulatory authority over Massachusetts coastal waters. The appeals court also found no dormant commerce clause violation, concluding that limiting the fishermen to either the state or federal programs as a remedy for over fishing was a legitimate use of state police power to conserve resources. There was no equal protection violation either, as the regulation was enacted to address overfishing in Massachusetts coastal waters and the adverse effect of fishers taking advantage of the loophole between the federal and state permitting schemes.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Chown

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Barnstable. Indictment found and returned in the Superior Court Department on May 12, 2006. A pretrial motion to suppress evidence was heard by Richard F. Connon, J. An application for leave to prosecute an interlocutory appeal was allowed by Margot Botsford, J., in the Supreme Judicial Court for the county of Suffolk, and the appeal was reported by her to the Appeals Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was arrested for operating without a driver's license and also charged with not having his registration in his possession and speeding. Defendant filed a motion to suppress evidence discovered during an inventory search. The Barnstable Superior Court Department (Massachusetts) granted the motion to suppress. The Commonwealth appealed.


OVERVIEW: A police stopped defendant's truck after he was observed speeding. Defendant presented the officer with a driver's license from New Brunswick, Canada, but could not produce the truck's registration. The officer had personal prior knowledge that defendant had previously had a Massachusetts driver's license and a check of the registry record revealed a lengthy history of in-state motor vehicle violations, with various suspensions and reinstatements. On appeal, the Commonwealth argued that the motion judge erred in granting the motion to suppress, because the officer reasonably believed that defendant was a Massachusetts residence driving without a license and thus, had probable cause to arrest him. The court concluded that because the police officer reasonably believed that defendant was a resident of Massachusetts and needed a Massachusetts driver's license to drive there, probable cause to arrest for a violation of operating a motor vehicle without a license existed. The stop being valid, drugs, money, and other evidence were appropriately seized.

Commonwealth v. Teixeira


No. 08-P-696.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


76 Mass. App. Ct. 101; 920 N.E.2d 56; 2010 Mass. App. LEXIS 28


April 7, 2009, Argued 
January 12, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Commonwealth v. Teixeira, 456 Mass. 1102, 922 N.E.2d 154, 2010 Mass. LEXIS 181 (2010)
Appeal after remand at, Decision reached on appeal by Commonwealth v. Teixeira, 2010 Mass. App. LEXIS 1339 (Mass. App. Ct., Oct. 15, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Middlesex. Complaint received and sworn to in the Cambridge Division of the District Court Department on December 26, 2006. A motion to dismiss was heard by Michele B. Hogan, J., and the case was tried before her.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Cambridge Division of the District Court Department (Massachusetts) convicted defendant of malicious destruction of property with a value less than $ 250, Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 266, § 127, and intimidation of a witness, Mass. Gen. Laws ch. 268, § 13B(1). After the verdict was returned, the trial court denied defendant's motion to dismiss and motion for mistrial. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that, the day before trial, the uncle of a Commonwealth witness, a police detective, had made threats to him which made him afraid to testify. The detective did not deny that the encounter took place, but contradicted defendant's version of it. The trial judge stated that she did not credit defendant's testimony that his interaction with the detective made him too fearful to testify. The appellate court found that the incident amounted to the sort of egregious misconduct that had provided a basis for remedial action in prior cases. Mere disbelief of defendant did not satisfy the Commonwealth's burden of showing that no interference actually occurred. However, the evidence supported a finding that the incident did not cause defendant to forgo his right to testify. Although defendant moved for a mistrial, no trial activity goaded him into doing so. Further, the Commonwealth showed that a fair trial was still possible. However, defendant's version of events showed misconduct so grossly improper that dismissal might have been appropriate even in the absence of prejudice. Further findings were essential to a proper disposition of defendant's motions.


OUTCOME: The orders denying defendant's motion for a mistrial and his motion to dismiss were vacated. The case was remanded for reconsideration, first, of the motion for a mistrial. If the mistrial motion was allowed, then the trial court was to reconsider the motion to dismiss. In either case, and without limitation, express findings on the content of the conversation between defendant and the detective during their encounter were essential.

Commonwealth v. McGee


No. 08-P-938.


APPEALS COURT OF MASSACHUSETTS


75 Mass. App. Ct. 499; 915 N.E.2d 235; 2009 Mass. App. LEXIS 1247


March 4, 2009, Argued 
October 20, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by Commonwealth v. McGee, 455 Mass. 1105, 918 N.E.2d 90, 2009 Mass. LEXIS 963 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Suffolk. Indictment found and returned in the Superior Court Department on November 13, 2003. The case was tried before Thomas E. Connolly, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Suffolk Superior Court Department (Massachusetts) convicted defendant of rape as a lesser included offense of aggravated rape, pursuant to Mass. Gen. Laws Ann. ch. 265, § 22. Defendant appealed, claiming that the Commonwealth violated the "first complaint" rule.


OVERVIEW: In a pretrial motion in limine, the Commonwealth contended that even though the complainant's neighbor was the first individual to whom she reported that she had been raped, another witness, the investigating officer who responded when the neighbor called the police, should have been substituted as the Commonwealth's designated first complaint witness. Over defendant's objection, the trial court allowed the officer to testify as the designated first complaint witness, on the ground that the officer had spoken with the complainant only a short time after she spoke with her neighbor, and had heard more details of the incident. The Commonwealth was not entitled to substitute a more detailed subsequent complaint merely because it strengthened the Commonwealth's case. The testimony of the complainant's neighbor was that the complainant reported having been sexually assaulted. The testimony plainly qualified as first complaint, and there was no evidence to suggest the neighbor was incompetent or biased. Indeed, her testimony closely matched what the complainant remembered telling her. The officer's more detailed testimony could have influenced the jury.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment.

May 6, 2009, Argued 
September 4, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by, in part Commonwealth v. Smith, 456 Mass. 1101, 922 N.E.2d 153, 2010 Mass. LEXIS 150 (2010)
Affirmed by Commonwealth v. Smith, 458 Mass. 1012, 935 N.E.2d 770, 2010 Mass. LEXIS 762 (2010)
Post-conviction relief granted at, in part, Post-conviction relief denied at, in part, Remanded by Commonwealth v. Smith, 2016 Mass. App. LEXIS 125 (Mass. App. Ct., Sept. 15, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Suffolk. Indictments found and returned in the Superior Court Department on May 3, 2005. The cases were tried before Thomas E. Connolly, J.
United States v. Smith, 101 F.3d 202, 1996 U.S. App. LEXIS 30976 (1st Cir. Mass., 1996)



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Suffolk Superior Court (Massachusetts) convicted defendant of armed home invasion under Mass. Gen. Laws Ann. ch. 265, § 18C, armed assault with intent to murder under Mass. Gen. Laws Ann. ch. 265, § 18(b), and unlawful possession of a firearm under Mass. Gen. Laws Ann. ch. 269, § 10(a). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that there was insufficient evidence of his guilt for home invasion because the indictment only charged one of the two alternate ways of proving the crime, specifically, that he entered the dwelling without knowledge of anyone inside. Therefore, he argued the Commonwealth could only prove home invasion by showing he had no knowledge of occupants in the dwelling. The appellate court disagreed, noting that the statute contained an element with alternative scienter clauses, not two separate offenses. The indictment merged the two scienter elements into one without affecting what the Commonwealth had to prove; namely, that prior to assaulting the occupant defendant knew or should have known of the occupant's presence. The appellate court also found that the Commonwealth provided sufficient evidence defendant entered the dwelling when his foot crossed the threshold of the door and when the gun was inserted into the apartment. The appellate court found that the victim's description of the gun as 11 or 12 inches in length was sufficient for the judge to deny defendant's motion for a required finding of not guilty on the firearm charge.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.
